  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_45dab063-314d-4252-b69d-1a603963932b'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

<span id="freeTextContainer17096891698688772487">5/1/14 EDIT: Sooo I stopped checking comments for months, and I come back to either rage or agreement with regard to my review. All I have to say is <b>THERE BE SPOILERS AHEAD</b>. Also, if you're on the fence about whether or not to continue reading this book, <b>don't read this review unless you're set on quitting</b>. <br/><br/>Okay? Okay. Onwards to the actual review!<br/><br/>*************<br/><br/>I just couldn't finish it. And I tried. Really, really tried. Even after studying Chinese history extensively in college, I'm usually not</span>
  <span id="freeText17096891698688772487" style="display:none">5/1/14 EDIT: Sooo I stopped checking comments for months, and I come back to either rage or agreement with regard to my review. All I have to say is <b>THERE BE SPOILERS AHEAD</b>. Also, if you're on the fence about whether or not to continue reading this book, <b>don't read this review unless you're set on quitting</b>. <br /><br />Okay? Okay. Onwards to the actual review!<br /><br />*************<br /><br />I just couldn't finish it. And I tried. Really, really tried. Even after studying Chinese history extensively in college, I'm usually not very picky about the books that I read. I don't really care about the accuracy or the sequence of events, as long as it's a good story. <br /><br />But maybe it's the cheesy title. Or maybe it's just too girly. Or maybe, just maybe, the fact that this foolish girl starved herself to death because of lovesickness was the last kick in the butt my usually sleepy (yet very existent) inner feminist could endure before waking and wanting to punch someone. I have enough idiots in my life to deal with. Please don't make me deal with more in my head. Granted, the book does somewhat cleverly depict the Chinese afterlife, but the rest was so <i>blah</i> and a little insulting to my intelligence and femininity. If I rolled my eyes any harder while reading this book, I would have fallen out of my chair. <br /><br />I may return to skim through the pages again to finish but probably not anytime soon. </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1939493049" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9547742369944134666">3 out of 5 stars to <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/713.Lisa_See' title='Lisa See' rel='nofollow noopener'>Lisa See</a>'s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24472.Peony_in_Love' title='Peony in Love by Lisa See' rel='nofollow noopener'>Peony in Love</a>, a historical fiction book released in 2007 by Random House set in 17th century China.<br/><br/><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1493692917ra/22642705.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><b>
  <i>
    <u>Why This Book</u>
  </i>
</b><br/>I found it sitting on a bookshelf in my condo's laundry room. I read the jacket description, which sounded like a beautiful tale of love, emotions and a little bit of history. I brought it home with me that afternoon, knowing it would come in handy. And when I finished up a few ARCs, I needed a different kind of book; I saw this on my own she</span>
  <span id="freeText9547742369944134666" style="display:none">3 out of 5 stars to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/713.Lisa_See" title="Lisa See" rel="nofollow noopener">Lisa See</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24472.Peony_in_Love" title="Peony in Love by Lisa See" rel="nofollow noopener">Peony in Love</a>, a historical fiction book released in 2007 by Random House set in 17th century China.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1493692917ra/22642705.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>
  <i>
    <u>Why This Book</u>
  </i>
</b><br>I found it sitting on a bookshelf in my condo's laundry room. I read the jacket description, which sounded like a beautiful tale of love, emotions and a little bit of history. I brought it home with me that afternoon, knowing it would come in handy. And when I finished up a few ARCs, I needed a different kind of book; I saw this on my own shelf, which reminded me it was time to try something a few years old. I picked it up and began reading last week. It took longer than usual, but I'll explain why later.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <u>Overview of Story</u>
  </i>
</b><br>The Peony Pavilion is a play that the character of Peony has read many times. When her father, of some wealth in 17th century China, puts on a showing of the famous play on his estate, 15-year old Peony is excited. But it's when she sees a boy for the first time, she cannot control her thoughts. Unfortunately, she's already paired off in an arranged marriage with a boy from another family, as well as the fact that as a girl, she's not even allowed to be seen with any males other than those in her own family.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1493692918ra/22642708.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The book follows the story line of the play, which seems to be spilling over into Peony's life. When she begins unknowingly starving herself, Peony dies and enters the afterworld on her own. She's unprepared to deal with the consequences and is remorseful that she never found love. She soon sees the boy she fell in love with in a dream, learning he was the man her father had arranged in the marriage. She longs for him but cannot have him, as she is dead and he is very much alive.<br><br>Soon, her family members begin dying and join her in the afterworld. The boy moves on and gets married. Peony inserts herself to their life from the great beyond, leading to unfortunate circumstances for all involved. As she meanders her journey, Peony learns what is needed for her to move beyond the "waiting place" and into her new existence as no longer alive.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1493692917ra/22642706.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>
  <i>
    <u>Approach &amp; Style</u>
  </i>
</b><br>1. The book is centered around a play within the book which mirrors the main character's life. At times, it's a little difficult to tell which is real life and which is the play.<br><br>2. The language is very ethereal, flowery and imaginative. This is less about plot and more about the beauty of Chinese beliefs about what women are allowed to do, what happens in death, and how to live one's life.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <u>Strengths</u>
  </i>
</b><br>1. The love story is a strong one. You see and feel the poetry in the words and the relationships.<br><br>2. It's very descriptive of life in a warrior state in 17th century China. I learned a lot of history that I wasn't privy to beforehand.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1493692917ra/22642707.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>3. You see everything thru Peony's eyes, which helps create a very strong world and point of view.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <u>Open Questions &amp; Concerns</u>
  </i>
</b><br>I am shocked at what Chinese women were put thru... between the sacrifices women made for men, the binding of feet and the cultural expectations and limitations. It was very upsetting. I understand these were customs for hundreds of years, with deep-rooted beliefs... some are just awful from today's standards. Even awful back then.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1493692918ra/22642709.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>
  <i>
    <u>Author &amp; Other Similar Books</u>
  </i>
</b><br>This is the first book about Chinese customs and history that I've read. I'm not sure what I could compare it to.... perhaps Memoirs of a Geisha, although it's a different country and belief system.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <u>Final Thoughts</u>
  </i>
</b><br>This was a very tough read. I started it ten days ago and read 20 pages. I tried a few times, but couldn't get into it. I forced myself to read 150 pages last night and then the remaining 100 today. It got better, but it wasn't a positive read for me; however, I recognize the beauty in the story, characters, imagery and setting. It's one of those books where I didn't like it a lot, but I know it's a good book.<br><br>I wish I had more knowledge of Chinese history and customs. Unfortunately, much of what happens in the book and how it's described went over my head. I didn't agree with how people felt or were treated. I didn't know why there was so much of a belief in ghosts with a vengeance. I couldn't get into religious and spiritual connections that were unfamiliar. And when I was getting close, I felt angry over how awfully these women were treated.<br><br>That said, I believe I would have liked this a lot more if I had a stronger background in the topics. The writing is good. The story is pretty. It's just a weak connection for me because I was unfamiliar with the core practices, history and belief systems. But for the right reader, it will probably be a good 3 to 4 rated book. For me, it was about a 2.5, and I rounded up to a 3 to be fair.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <u>About Me</u>
  </i>
</b><br>For those new to me or my reviews... here's the scoop: I read A LOT. I write A LOT. And now I blog A LOT. First the book review goes on Goodreads, and then I send it on over to my WordPress blog at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://thisismytruthnow.com">https://thisismytruthnow.com</a>, where you'll also find TV &amp; Film reviews, the revealing and introspective 365 Daily Challenge and lots of blogging about places I've visited all over the world. And you can find all my social media profiles to get the details on the who/what/when/where and my pictures. Leave a comment and let me know what you think. Vote in the poll and ratings. Thanks for stopping by. <i>Note</i>: All written content is my original creation and copyrighted to me, but the graphics and images were linked from other sites and belong to them. Many thanks to their original creators.<br><br>[polldaddy poll=9729544]<br>	<br>[polldaddy poll=9719251]</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15142042264011593615"><br/><b> <i> “"When people are alive they love, when they die, they keep loving. If love ends when a person dies, that is not real love" </i></b> <br/><br/>I have a confession to make. I bought this book not by choice but by mere economic benefit. The paperback (the brand new one still wrapped in cellophane) was on sale and I bought it for a very fairly cheap price. Guess how much. <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[ One U.S. dollar, can you believe that!!??  <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br/><br/>I was elated to discover that this is a historical novel as I made a </span>
  <span id="freeText15142042264011593615" style="display:none"><br /><b> <i> “"When people are alive they love, when they die, they keep loving. If love ends when a person dies, that is not real love" </i></b> <br /><br />I have a confession to make. I bought this book not by choice but by mere economic benefit. The paperback (the brand new one still wrapped in cellophane) was on sale and I bought it for a very fairly cheap price. Guess how much. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ One U.S. dollar, can you believe that!!??  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />I was elated to discover that this is a historical novel as I made a recent promise to myself to increase my historical fiction reads. I dove right into the book and read every page as fast as I possibly could despite the challenge of going through realistic historical events set in 17th century China that kept threatening to put me to sleep. Luckily, I upped my dose of caffeine, so I was able to finish the book in less than two days. Yay! <br /><br />This is mainly a love story or rather a story of love-sickness and in the point of view of a practical, modern woman, it would be the most foolish thing to starve yourself because of love and yes I am one of those women. Had it ended in that tragic death, I would say it would be just an overused lovesick tragic tale. <br /><br />But it didn’t end there! The latter half of the story which I found very hauntingly fantastic presents the MC’s journey in the afterlife as her love for her one and only man transcends even death. <br /><br />But far better than the story itself, I felt a greater pull toward the imaginative, vivid, and evocative writing of the author and the way she perfectly depicted ancient Chinese cultures and rituals on courtship, marriage and death and I found myself captivated. <br /><br />Even though, I wasn’t a huge fan of the story, I highly commend <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/713.Lisa_See" title="Lisa See" rel="nofollow noopener">Lisa See</a> for the brilliant artist that she is by writing this priceless artifact of a novel. <br /></span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Becky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Becky" href="/user/show/93420-becky">Becky</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17267056032571339990"><br/><br/><i>Set in 17th-century China, See’s fifth novel is a coming-of-age story, a ghost story, a family saga and a work of musical and social history. As Peony, the 15-year-old daughter of the wealthy Chen family, approaches an arranged marriage, she commits an unthinkable breach of etiquette when she accidentally comes upon a man who has entered the family garden. Unusually for a girl of her time, Peony has been educated and revels in studying The Peony Pavilion, a real opera published in 1598, as the r</i></span>
  <span id="freeText17267056032571339990" style="display:none"><br /><br /><i>Set in 17th-century China, See’s fifth novel is a coming-of-age story, a ghost story, a family saga and a work of musical and social history. As Peony, the 15-year-old daughter of the wealthy Chen family, approaches an arranged marriage, she commits an unthinkable breach of etiquette when she accidentally comes upon a man who has entered the family garden. Unusually for a girl of her time, Peony has been educated and revels in studying The Peony Pavilion, a real opera published in 1598, as the repercussions of the meeting unfold. The novel’s plot mirrors that of the opera, and eternal themes abound: an intelligent girl chafing against the restrictions of expected behavior; fiction’s educative powers; the rocky path of love between lovers and in families. It figures into the plot that generations of young Chinese women, known as the lovesick maidens, became obsessed with The Peony Pavilion, and, in a Werther-like passion, many starved themselves to death. See (Snow Flower and the Secret Fan, etc.) offers meticulous depiction of women’s roles in Qing and Ming dynasty China (including horrifying foot-binding scenes) and vivid descriptions of daily Qing life, festivals and rituals. Peony’s vibrant voice, perfectly pitched between the novel’s historical and passionate depths, carries her story beautifully—in life and afterlife.)</i> <br />I must say that I loved the first Lisa See novel that I read (Snow Flower and the Secret Fan) and had high hopes for this one, and was severely disappointed. It just rambled on and on (SPOILER) even after the main character dies. Uggh. After the beautiful writing of “Snow Flower”, it was hard to read this– it was simply missing too much - in terms of explanations, background details, character development, and tying together really choppy scenes, that it felt amateurish, trite and unmoving.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17267056032571339990" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eleyna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eleyna" href="/user/show/351431-eleyna">Eleyna</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone</span></div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6087559" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13255301111756580628">This book is amazing!!! It is beautifully written (lovely descriptions and intricate details), well researched, and unbelievably touching. There were moments where I feel I can't read anymore because I am so overwhelmed with emotion, but I can never put the book down for any real length of time. I have become completely engrossed.   <span id="freeText13255301111756580628" style="display:none">This book is amazing!!! It is beautifully written (lovely descriptions and intricate details), well researched, and unbelievably touching. There were moments where I feel I can't read anymore because I am so overwhelmed with emotion, but I can never put the book down for any real length of time. I have become completely engrossed. The summary of the book is a little misleading. It describes the book as a love story. I do not find it to be one. Peony in Love is a romantic and tragic story. Love plays a big part, but not necessarily in the way I expected it to. More important in this book are the facts. 1)The traditions and beliefs practiced in the book were actually the beliefs and practices of the time, and some are really bizarre to me! 2)A woman's place in the society of that age. It's fascinating to see the differences from then to now, and also to realize that while there are differences, there are also many similarities today in women's oppression and sensoring as there were then. 3)The women in the story were real women, as was Wu Ren. Their comments on the play The Peony Pavilion are real and were compiled into a book: Wu Wushan’s Three Wives’ Collaborative Commentary of The Peony Pavilion. I don't want to give too much away. Please read it for yourself. One way or another you will be touched. It is a truly amazing read! </span>
  <a data-text-id="13255301111756580628" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_54985789" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/54985789" />
    <a title="Grace Tjan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1889855-grace-tjan"><img alt="Grace Tjan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307595874p2/1889855.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/54985789?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Grace Tjan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Grace Tjan" href="/user/show/1889855-grace-tjan">Grace Tjan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 9780747594949" href="/book/show/3218287-peony-in-love">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1889855-grace-tjan?shelf=china">china</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1889855-grace-tjan?shelf=2009">2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1889855-grace-tjan?shelf=long-ago-and-never-was">long-ago-and-never-was</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer54985789" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11949849038926792398">'My eyes were shaped like bamboo leaves; my brows were like gentle brushstrokes limned by a calligrapher'.<br/><br/>The notion that 17th century Chinese characters would speak like this for the benefit of 21st century English-speaking readers is preposterous to say the least. It is obvious that the author has conducted extensive research on the history and culture of late Ming/ early Qing dynasty Hangzhou, and the fruits of that research abound in this historical-supernatural novel. However, the anthropol</span>
  <span id="freeText11949849038926792398" style="display:none">'My eyes were shaped like bamboo leaves; my brows were like gentle brushstrokes limned by a calligrapher'.<br /><br />The notion that 17th century Chinese characters would speak like this for the benefit of 21st century English-speaking readers is preposterous to say the least. It is obvious that the author has conducted extensive research on the history and culture of late Ming/ early Qing dynasty Hangzhou, and the fruits of that research abound in this historical-supernatural novel. However, the anthropological details, more often than not, tend to choke the life out of the characters and sometimes even the narrative itself. <br /><br />The historical fact behind the story itself is interesting, namely that the cultural renaissance that took place in late Ming dynasty Hangzhou gave aristocratic Chinese women unprecedented access to education, and even enabled them to actively participate in the literary scene. Three of these women, all the wives of Wu Ren, wrote The Three Wives Commentary on the Peony Pavillion, apparently the 'first book of its kind to have been published anywhere in the world to have been written by women', according to the author. However, instead of telling us the story of how these women managed to write such a work, the author chose to wallow in questionable exoticism and new-agey philosophizing. The emphasis on exoticism, some of which seem to be contrived for the benefit of Western audiences, reduces the complexity and richness of Chinese culture into a set of cruel customs and absurd superstitions.<br /><br />SPOILERS<br /><br />Peony is an insipid 16-year old girl who wilfully starves herself to death after she was betrothed to a stranger, who later turns out to be her paramour, Wu Ren. For some undecipherable reason (morbidity?, stupidity?), she never bothers to inquire about the identity of her fiancee until it is too late. Due to some sort of a shenanigan involving her ancestral tablet, she is condemned to spend her time wandering the afterlife as a hungry ghost. This exploration of the Chinese afterlife is interesting for a while, but it quickly degenerates into a mushy ghost story when Peony decides to stalk her former fiancee. With her ghostly powers, she interferes in his life, including choosing and grooming a three-year old girl for his future wife. The ick factor multiplies when we learn that she also participates (in her ghostly way) in the couple's bedroom activities. Finally, after further trials and tribulations, she is released from her status as a hungry ghost to join her revered ancestors in heaven.<br /><br />Two stars for the story and writing, an extra star for the research.<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11949849038926792398" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_5550899" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5550899" />
    <a title="Caroline" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/244267-caroline"><img alt="Caroline" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1332624023p2/244267.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/5550899?book_show_action=true">Sep 02, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline" href="/user/show/244267-caroline">Caroline</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/244267-caroline?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer5550899" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2934615087245158663">Peony is a 15-year-old girl in Manchu China who falls in love with the opera <i>The Peony Pavilion</i>, a love story in which the ghost of a girl is brought back to life by the man she loves. Peony's father hosts a production of the opera (which Peony assumes is for her, rather than to impress the powerful men that her father has visiting), and Peony meets a young man three nights in a row. She falls in love with him, but at the end of the third night they know they both must go on to their arranged ma</span>
  <span id="freeText2934615087245158663" style="display:none">Peony is a 15-year-old girl in Manchu China who falls in love with the opera <i>The Peony Pavilion</i>, a love story in which the ghost of a girl is brought back to life by the man she loves. Peony's father hosts a production of the opera (which Peony assumes is for her, rather than to impress the powerful men that her father has visiting), and Peony meets a young man three nights in a row. She falls in love with him, but at the end of the third night they know they both must go on to their arranged marriages and will most likely never see each other again. Peony becomes obsessed with the opera, and stops eating almost completely in her want to write a complete commentary on it.<br /><br />Anything more will spoil significant parts of the plot, but I'll say that it heavily involves ghosts and the Chinese afterworld. It's really a fascinating read, both to get an idea of the "lovesick girls" (who actually did exist--girls who wasted away, pining for love after reading <i>The Peony Pavilion</i>) and to get a better understanding of old Chinese ghosts and death rituals. It's a little fantastical at times, but it definitely not so much that it overtakes the core of the novel: a woman's craving to be heard.<br /><br />Definitely nothing like her previous novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1103.Snow_Flower_and_the_Secret_Fan" title="Snow Flower and the Secret Fan by Lisa See" rel="nofollow noopener">Snow Flower and the Secret Fan</a>, but I think that's what made it so great. It gives a good understanding of other aspects of Chinese culture, in a time where women were trying to have their creative voices heard rather than continuing their quiet existence in their family's inner chambers. Very beautiful story!</span>
  <a data-text-id="2934615087245158663" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5037472155296122312">The first time I had contact with this book, was by listening to it on my way to work. As soon as it was done, I ran out and bought the book. I just *needed* to view the words that I had heard on the audiobook. As soon as I finished reading the last page, I turned to page 1 and started over again. This is the first and only time that I have ever done that. This is one of my all time favorite books, and I keep a copy beside my bed, so it is never far from my mind...</span>

<div id="review_22889250" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22889250" />
    <a title="YoSafBridg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/440929-yosafbridg"><img alt="YoSafBridg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1211664027p2/440929.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/22889250?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="YoSafBridg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="YoSafBridg" href="/user/show/440929-yosafbridg">YoSafBridg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer22889250" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11047269799793598031">As i was making my way through the first section of Peony in Love, i was beginning to think i should have paid more heed to the valentine heart on its spine (my library system's way of signifying that it was of the romance genre) because Peony was mooning like a lovesick girl who knew not enough of the world and it was all just a little overmuch for me.<br/><br/>But then she died, and it got so much better. I had rather eagerly anticipated this novel, because i had loved Lisa See's last, <i>Snow Flower and t</i></span>
  <span id="freeText11047269799793598031" style="display:none">As i was making my way through the first section of Peony in Love, i was beginning to think i should have paid more heed to the valentine heart on its spine (my library system's way of signifying that it was of the romance genre) because Peony was mooning like a lovesick girl who knew not enough of the world and it was all just a little overmuch for me.<br /><br />But then she died, and it got so much better. I had rather eagerly anticipated this novel, because i had loved Lisa See's last, <i>Snow Flower and the Secret Fan</i>.<br /><br />Set shortly after the fall of the Ming Dynasty in seventeenth century China and based on real historic characters and works of literature, Peony in Love is an involving tale of an often unknown period of women writers.<br /><br />Soon to celebrate her sixteenth birthday (on the the rather auspicious Double Seven~the seventh day of the seventh month~for which her father has commissioned a very special performance of the opera The Peony Pavilion, her very favorite~and scandalously enough women will be allowed to watch, though from behind a screen.) Peony is also about to embark on young womanhood, is betrothed to a man she has not met (as Chinese tradition of the day dictated) and will soon be marrying out. On the first night of the three-night opera she leaves to take some air and meets a young poet who reminds her of the hero of the opera. It is, but of course, love at first sight. Peony is destined to pine away for her young poet and die of love-sickness before she can be married as have so many young girls before her~victims just like the heroine the idolize in the The Peony Pavilion.<br /><br />It is only after death that Peony can begin to see the world and her family for what they really are, even though these perceptions go through a couple of changes even then. She continues her growth process even after death. After death she learns of the relationships her family members had with each other, as well as changing her views about the relationships she had with them. She develops a relationship with the grandmother she has always worshiped and respected as an ancestor and learns to see her as a real woman. Peony grows into a woman who makes mistakes and longs to be heard (a problem of many women as they moved from the slightly more liberal Ming dynasty to the more repressive Manchus, even more so for Peony facing the challenges of being a ghost), she also grows to truly love her husband in death and learns to recognize the difference between that and what she felt as a young girl. Though many of the elements of this tale i could see coming i still enjoyed the process of reading about them (and isn't that what it's all about anyway~and there really aren't any new tales to tell~isn't that part of the point?)<br /><br />See does a beautiful job of depicting the turmoil of the teenage heart, as well as the problems faced with growing older and facing our mistakes, though she did it in a novel way. She also illuminated a Chinese belief system that i knew nothing about. I deeply enjoyed this novel and gained an even greater appreciation for See's artistry. It made me want to learn more about The Three Wives Commentary, the original inspiration for this novel, which was the "first book of its kind to been published anywhere in the world to have been written by women" (the three consecutive wives of Wu Ren) as well as bringing back memories of the wonderful but haunting <i>Woman Warrior: Memoirs of a Girlhood Among Ghosts</i> by the incomparable Maxine Hong Kingston. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11047269799793598031" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_41428314" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41428314" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41428314?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=miscellaneous-fiction">miscellaneous-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=read-only-cos-of-gr-friends">read-only-cos-of-gr-friends</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=china-japan-asia">china-japan-asia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41428314" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16876782849553525638">An interesting story, with a clever structure, weaving parallels between the legend of the Peony Pavilion with the "real" story of a girl called Peony, though that does make the first third very predictable.<br/><br/>I found the faux naive voice of Peony a little irritating at times; it is too didactic in the way it fills readers in on the historical detail of customs and beliefs, being more like a factual book than a novel. Whilst I found all that interesting, I think it could have been presented more su</span>
  <span id="freeText16876782849553525638" style="display:none">An interesting story, with a clever structure, weaving parallels between the legend of the Peony Pavilion with the "real" story of a girl called Peony, though that does make the first third very predictable.<br /><br />I found the faux naive voice of Peony a little irritating at times; it is too didactic in the way it fills readers in on the historical detail of customs and beliefs, being more like a factual book than a novel. Whilst I found all that interesting, I think it could have been presented more subtly (or else with footnotes). Still, it was interesting to consider that footbinding could actually be an act or rebellion and even freedom for poorer girls (as in Snow Flower too).<br /><br />For much of the book I was concerned that the message was that reading is a dangerous activity for women, though that is finally overturned – sort of. <br /><br />There were incongruous bits that sounded too modern and that a good editor should have picked up on as they were needlessly distracting: some of the descriptions of the empowering of women; lovesickness often sounded too much like a modern analysis of anorexia - explicitly when Ze says "I stopped eating, and for once I had total control over my destiny", and the ghastly line stolen from a budget sci fi movie "We asked the netherworld bureaucrats and received one time return-to-earth permits".<br /><br />I also found the "clouds and rain" threesome/intimacy by proxy, a rather disquieting concept. Although I realise it was meant to be a beautiful coming together, it rather tainted the rest of the story for me.<br /><br />So overall, interesting, but for me, a frustrating and disappointing read, albeit quite educational. It only gets as many as 3 stars because I like reading things that evoke China.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16876782849553525638" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2545365734" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2545365734?book_show_action=true">Oct 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Debby" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Debby" href="/user/show/56322436-debby">Debby</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56322436-debby?shelf=authors-s-t">authors-s-t</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2545365734" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14379671429741014237">Audiobook Review.<br/><br/>I've never read or listened to anything by Lisa See, so this was my first for one of my club's challenge. The story is set in Manchu, China and it is of a girl named Peony who is now of marriagable age. She is 15-years old and is fascinated and in love with the opera, The Peony Pavilion. This opera happens to be hosted by her father and is about a ghost of a girl who is brought back to life by the man she loves. Peony believes it is for her rather than the elite group of men vis</span>
  <span id="freeText14379671429741014237" style="display:none">Audiobook Review.<br /><br />I've never read or listened to anything by Lisa See, so this was my first for one of my club's challenge. The story is set in Manchu, China and it is of a girl named Peony who is now of marriagable age. She is 15-years old and is fascinated and in love with the opera, The Peony Pavilion. This opera happens to be hosted by her father and is about a ghost of a girl who is brought back to life by the man she loves. Peony believes it is for her rather than the elite group of men visiting her father.<br /><br />During the opera, Peony meets a young man and falls in love with him, but eventually they have to come to the realization that they must move on and to the marriages that have been arranged for them and never see each other ever again. Unfortunately, Peony becomes obsessed with the opera and practically starves herself in her need to write her thoughts on the opera. She eventually dies and enters the afterworld and suffers the consequences of never finding true love herself. In her dreams, she learns that the man she had fell in love with was the same person her father had arranged for her to marry. Thus starts her longing to be with him but she can't because she is dead and he is alive.<br /><br />This story gets more into the afterlife not only for her but for family members as they join her when they depart the living. It also tells of Peony's interrupting the life of the man she loved as he has married and moved on. I am not a big fan of afterlife/ghosts type novels, but I found this novel a fascinating listen. Not sure if I will read more from this author but thought is was a very well written and nice listen.<br /><br />3-Stars</span>
  <a data-text-id="14379671429741014237" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_347612416" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/347612416" />
    <a title="Denise" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8887111-denise"><img alt="Denise" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1476297215p2/8887111.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/347612416?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Denise" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Denise" href="/user/show/8887111-denise">Denise</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer347612416" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11700224326996321869"><br/>Lisa See's writing is beautiful, and I understand this book is supposed to be about the women of China who wanted their voices to be heard. But what I found really fascinating about it was the descriptions of Chinese rituals and superstitions, and learning what the Chinese thought about death. The descriptions were so vivid to me that I felt like I could really see the pavilions, the plum tree, the Viewing Terrace. <br/>There were so many sentences that made me reflect about life in general. "My hear</span>
  <span id="freeText11700224326996321869" style="display:none"><br />Lisa See's writing is beautiful, and I understand this book is supposed to be about the women of China who wanted their voices to be heard. But what I found really fascinating about it was the descriptions of Chinese rituals and superstitions, and learning what the Chinese thought about death. The descriptions were so vivid to me that I felt like I could really see the pavilions, the plum tree, the Viewing Terrace. <br />There were so many sentences that made me reflect about life in general. "My heart is empty and my life has no value anymore. Each moment is a thousand tears." " No one can exist without joy, anger, grief, fear, love, hate and desire. The Seven Emotions are what makes us human." This is a well written historical fiction novel.<br /></span>

<div id="review_14740519" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/14740519" />
    <a title="Marissa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/876626-marissa"><img alt="Marissa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1233781932p2/876626.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/14740519?book_show_action=true">Feb 06, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marissa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marissa" href="/user/show/876626-marissa">Marissa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14740519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3082243756135924126">I really enjoyed reading Snow Flower and the Secret Fan. Peony in Love has a few of the same elements but is, nicely, a different novel. Peony is the only child of a wealthy couple who experienced some hard times during the Manchu raids. Peony, encouraged by her father, loves to read. She reads extensively but her favorite work is the Peony Pavilion--an opera. Her father stages the opera at her home and the women are allowed to watch through a screen. Peony meets a boy, falls in love, and begins</span>
  <span id="freeText3082243756135924126" style="display:none">I really enjoyed reading Snow Flower and the Secret Fan. Peony in Love has a few of the same elements but is, nicely, a different novel. Peony is the only child of a wealthy couple who experienced some hard times during the Manchu raids. Peony, encouraged by her father, loves to read. She reads extensively but her favorite work is the Peony Pavilion--an opera. Her father stages the opera at her home and the women are allowed to watch through a screen. Peony meets a boy, falls in love, and begins to follow some of the same experiences as the main character in the opera. Convinced that she'll be marrying someone other than the boy she met, she succumbs to "lovesickness" and slowly starves herself to death. Mix-ups happen and Peony's spirit isn't able to move on since her tablet hasn't been dotted yet. A good portion of the novel is her experiences as her ghost waits to be able to move on in the afterlife.<br /><br />I enjoyed the novel. I don't know a lot of the Chinese beliefs and other traditions, just bits here and there. It was fascinating learning a bit of what the Chinese afterlife would have been like. On dying, Peony's spirit splits into three pieces, one of which is to go into her tablet to stay with her family. As her tablet hasn't been dotted, Peony becomes a 'Hungry ghost." Fascinating.<br /><br />Another cultural thing I found of interest was the lovesick maidens. While they are mentioned in the story, the comments at the end of the novel by Lisa See brought their stories more to mind. See mentions that in Chinese history, there were a number of girls who, like in the opera and Peony, become lovesick and starve themselves. The idea is introduced through the opera with the idea that the women would gain freedom and control over their lives in the afterlife and choose thier own husbands. This idea of power over oneself is so appealing that many women practiced physical control over their bodies by becoming anorexic to the point of death, possibly hoping for the chance for a choice in love in the afterlife.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3082243756135924126" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9953095383360307673">I'm usually a stoic person, and not prone to tearjerker moments, most of the time when I see a sad part in a movie or read a sad part in a book, I can simply read it and move on, however well it's written. But that was not the case with this book, and it serves as an example of how good this book is.<br/><br/>I already read Snow Flower and the Secret Fan, but I liked this book better. The writing style for both books is awesome, but I liked the story in this one better. Everything is so beautifully descri</span>
  <span id="freeText9953095383360307673" style="display:none">I'm usually a stoic person, and not prone to tearjerker moments, most of the time when I see a sad part in a movie or read a sad part in a book, I can simply read it and move on, however well it's written. But that was not the case with this book, and it serves as an example of how good this book is.<br /><br />I already read Snow Flower and the Secret Fan, but I liked this book better. The writing style for both books is awesome, but I liked the story in this one better. Everything is so beautifully described (It reminded me of Anchee Min's novel, 'Empress Orchid' prose-wise) so it's easy to imagine the surroundings and characters, and how everything is happening.<br /><br />I cried at the end of the first part, after I realized what had actually happened to Peony. That poor girl, after learning the truth about her husband and becoming so happy, only to have it all taken away. And then the ending made me cry too, damnit. I used up a good amount of Kleenex twice, after the first part and at the ending. This book is just so heartachingly beautiful and sad, I want to hate the book because it's so sad and I felt it was unfair to poor Peony after what she had been through, but it's also so beautifully written, with a bittersweet ending. </span>

<div id="review_740957162" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/740957162" />
    <a title="Kavita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5858699-kavita"><img alt="Kavita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1315387023p2/5858699.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/740957162?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kavita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kavita" href="/user/show/5858699-kavita">Kavita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=ghosts-and-horror">ghosts-and-horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=china">china</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5858699-kavita?shelf=wtf">wtf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer740957162" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13826409771950814720">This book is quite hard to classify and I cannot even decide whether I liked it or not. It could be historical fiction, but not quite. There has been excellent research on Chinese practices during the Ming dynasty, and Chinese beliefs in the afterlife have been depicted in detail. The book is basically set in the past, and is based on real people who lived and a real opera wrote in the 1500s. But most of the book is about a ghost, so historical fiction doesn’t quite fit.<br/><br/>Then this could be classi</span>
  <span id="freeText13826409771950814720" style="display:none">This book is quite hard to classify and I cannot even decide whether I liked it or not. It could be historical fiction, but not quite. There has been excellent research on Chinese practices during the Ming dynasty, and Chinese beliefs in the afterlife have been depicted in detail. The book is basically set in the past, and is based on real people who lived and a real opera wrote in the 1500s. But most of the book is about a ghost, so historical fiction doesn’t quite fit.<br /><br />Then this could be classified as feminist fiction, but not quite. To be sure, there are many feminist lessons to be learned from this book. Peony has led a cloistered life in an attempt by her parents to protect her but in the end, women cannot be ‘protected’ by others. They can only be taught to live life and defend themselves as best as they can. The book also deals with women’s education, which is often not considered as important as a man’s, even today. Women poets in China have existed for centuries but they have not been honoured in the same way as men poets. This is an important theme that the book focuses on. And finally, the danger of keeping your daughter away from any man in the hopes of selling her off in an arranged marriage is explored in detail. (Any book that shows arranged marriages as inhuman social constructions gets an extra star.) But then again, there are very unfeminist elements in the book such as Peony desiring both her husband’s and her parents’ families to have sons to carry on the lineage. Peony and her sister-wife-ghost also fight over Ren as if they were stupid teenagers. It was all about a man, and not about women’s desires. As a ghost, Peony could have been depicted as rising above petty traditions that discriminate against women, but she doesn’t. She remains a woman who strongly believes in female inferiority even as a ghost, when she is supposed to have been released from all earthly obligations. Also, everyone keeps sneering about Peony being a worthless branch of the family tree for the sole reason that she was female. So no, I cannot accept this as feminist fiction. <br /><br />And finally, it could be a love story. But considering that the main characters only meet each other THREE times, I can hardly consider it one. Besides, one character is dead for most of the book and they NEVER meet again.<br /><br />Basically, this book is about a girl who has absolutely no idea about life and no one would tell her anything about it either. She meets a man ONCE by mistake, and then falls in ‘love’. Errr ... okay! Then she starves herself to death over him, and her family throws her out to die because she must not die within the house since she is an unmarried woman. Whatever! And then begins the REAL weird part as Peony becomes a ghost and starts haunting her to-be husband, who marries another woman. She haunts the other wife and so on until she dies. And then she mothers a child by proxy and breaks her feet and gets her married off to Ren (WTF!) and then claims she has found motherly love with her sister wife! Gimme a break! And then, when evil second wife returns to harm innocent third wife, all hell breaks loose. And then, she is finally released when her husband takes care of the last rite. There is simply no point to the story once she becomes a ghost. And I certainly did not need to read about a ghost woman joining in the copulation between husband and wife and ... I just have no words. <br /><br />On the other hand, I enjoyed reading the book even though I was fuming most of the time over Peony’s stupidity. It’s just one of those books. 3 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13826409771950814720" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer686101668" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6666368277526412742">Young Peony was brought up to be a good daughter and wife. At her sixteenth birthday, she knows that her future is set but when she chances a meeting with a handsome man at a theater performance, she begins to feel a longing and desire that sets the course for this novel. <br/><br/>Originally, when I read the blurb of this book, I thought it had an incredibly interesting concept and was rather excited to read a novel that was so rich in Chinese culture and tradition, since I would be rather ignorant in th</span>
  <span id="freeText6666368277526412742" style="display:none">Young Peony was brought up to be a good daughter and wife. At her sixteenth birthday, she knows that her future is set but when she chances a meeting with a handsome man at a theater performance, she begins to feel a longing and desire that sets the course for this novel. <br /><br />Originally, when I read the blurb of this book, I thought it had an incredibly interesting concept and was rather excited to read a novel that was so rich in Chinese culture and tradition, since I would be rather ignorant in these matters. So naturally, I really wanted to like this book. It's quite unfortunate that the book had very few redeeming qualities.<br /><br />The character of Peony, I felt, was one that was very difficult to sympathise with. Her naivety, innocence and idealism can be dismissed as a result of her young age for a certain part of the novel but since it persists for the whole duration of it, it's easy to conclude that these things are deeply-entrenched character traits rather than anything else. There is growth in Peony's character during the novel, noticeable growth but none of that makes me like her more as a character. <br /><br />The writing itself is simplistic, which works for the book for the most part. However, a lot of the descriptions felt jarring and contrived, as if the author was purposely trying to be too poetic - to the point where the prose was actually slightly amusing as opposed to solemn or sincere.<br /><br />However, the plot itself was probably the biggest problem of the novel for me. Reading the Afterword of the book, Lisa See seems to believe that she has managed to capture some form of female historical resistance or empowerment through this piece of literature and whilst there are certain aspects of that, they are quickly overridden by a largely misogynistic narrative. I understand that See was attempting to tell the true story of these women but she admits to the lack of knowledge about the three wives of the Wu family. It seems that See invented much of what happened in the latter half of the book - the supernatural elements of it. So it is disappointing and slightly sickening actually, the sheer amount of disrespect given to women in the plot during this latter half. I felt this especially through the portrayal of Ze's character. She's made out to be a villain for the majority of the book, whilst we're supposed to be sympathise with the main character of Peony, despite the fact that Peony robbed Ze of her agency and her body. <br /><br />Whilst there were certain narratives within the overarching story that I enjoyed; such as the excursions of Peony's mother and grandmother (overridden by the Manchus invasion - I felt it eventually told a story about a woman's place being at home and punished the two characters for their attempt to traverse outside of their gender roles) and the Banana Garden Five, the overall story, although boasted of female empowerment and feminist ideologies, entirely failed in its promises.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6666368277526412742" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5308022050839665955">I read this book by accident, knew nothing about it, never read the author before and the book was there for the taking so I took it. It takes place in 17th century China. and describes the life of a 15 year old girl named Peony, in a affluent family, educated, cloistered and in love with the opera, The Peony Pavilion which I hope I can also get a copy of to read. Some would consider it a love story , some a coming of age, others fantasy , and also a ghost story. To me it was historical fiction </span>
  <span id="freeText5308022050839665955" style="display:none">I read this book by accident, knew nothing about it, never read the author before and the book was there for the taking so I took it. It takes place in 17th century China. and describes the life of a 15 year old girl named Peony, in a affluent family, educated, cloistered and in love with the opera, The Peony Pavilion which I hope I can also get a copy of to read. Some would consider it a love story , some a coming of age, others fantasy , and also a ghost story. To me it was historical fiction and I learned so much about ancient china culture. From the reviews that I have read since finishing you either love this book or hate it. Something no one mentioned but which I believe, is that in 17th century China there was a rash of cases of anorexia but they called it love sickness, occurring primarily in adolescents girls, and to me understandable because of the lack of any control over the destiny of your life. I loved this book, it moved at a slow pace but spanned many years and at least half of the book is told by a ghost but revealing so many cultural beliefs of the Chinese at that time. <br /><br />I am hopeful that the culture presented is accurate because nothing upsets me more than to later discover very little of what was presented was fabricated. </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812975222" href="/book/show/1935309.Peony_in_Love">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">historical fiction readers</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4870237-margie?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2043345559" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10867704407458908553">Peony in Love is a ghost story and a love story set in seventeenth-century China. The research that Lisa See put into this novel is formidable as always. The novel enlightens one about seventeenth century family relationships and ancestor veneration in China, beliefs about the afterlife, the history of a famous opera, "The Peony Pavilion" and the effects that it had on young women, the renaissance of women poets and writers during that era, foot-binding, marriage customs, the political scene at </span>
  <span id="freeText10867704407458908553" style="display:none">Peony in Love is a ghost story and a love story set in seventeenth-century China. The research that Lisa See put into this novel is formidable as always. The novel enlightens one about seventeenth century family relationships and ancestor veneration in China, beliefs about the afterlife, the history of a famous opera, "The Peony Pavilion" and the effects that it had on young women, the renaissance of women poets and writers during that era, foot-binding, marriage customs, the political scene at the time the Ming Dynasty fell and the Qing Dynasty took over, especially the effects of war on the population, and the life of the upper classes vs. that of the lower classes. This page turning novel of love, loss and family is also a ghost story that kept me up all night wanting to know the ending. <br /><br />Thank you again, Lisa See, for transporting me to another time and place and entertaining me with an engrossing history lesson.</span>

<div id="review_2094418450" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2094418450" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6770267-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417793778p2/6770267.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2094418450?book_show_action=true">Aug 16, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/6770267-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780812975222" href="/book/show/1935309.Peony_in_Love">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6770267-chris?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6770267-chris?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6770267-chris?shelf=china">china</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2094418450" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13808358185223664453">I wasn't sure how to categorize this book. It IS historical fiction and based on true events, but also has elements of fantasy as a big part is a ghost story. It also eked up to 4 stars for me at the end. The story really had parts that were fascinating and others that I just wanted the scenes to be over or the theme finished. Reading the Author's note and the Conversation with Lisa See, it brought out much more context & depth, which made me appreciate the structure and content of the novel mor</span>
  <span id="freeText13808358185223664453" style="display:none">I wasn't sure how to categorize this book. It IS historical fiction and based on true events, but also has elements of fantasy as a big part is a ghost story. It also eked up to 4 stars for me at the end. The story really had parts that were fascinating and others that I just wanted the scenes to be over or the theme finished. Reading the Author's note and the Conversation with Lisa See, it brought out much more context &amp; depth, which made me appreciate the structure and content of the novel more.<br /><br />What did I like? Learning more about the culture &amp; traditions of 17thC China. The themes of all kinds of love and virtues: romantic love, parental love, duty, loyalty, sacrifice and gratitude. The dichotomy of the lives of women: aspirational versus reality. I didn't really think about the secrets we keep and how it impacts us until reading a comment by the Author &amp; I realized how much the relationships were affected by a lack of full knowledge by various characters.<br /><br />I knew there was a story within a story as Chen Tong's (Peony) narrative was somewhat parallel to the opera The Peony Pavilion (which is a real opera) that was the catalyst for the initial storyline and a story of which she became obsessed with both in life and death. But I found it stretched credulity when she traveled the same path so quickly as the heroine in the opera. However, once again, the author's comments about how those who do not have control of their lives can feel in control aided my understanding of some characters actions. <br /><br />As a Westerner, the whole concept and details of the Chinese afterlife were mind blowing. So very real for the Chinese, but felt very much like fantasy to me. The ghost story is the bulk of the novel. Just as I write that I think, gee as a Christian, you believe in a spiritual world, so is this so fanciful?<br />Some of it -yes! Like helping Tan Ze get pregnant by moving the right "seed" into the ovum.... Get my drift? <br />Chen Tong continues to grow during her time as a hungry Ghost which I think is an unusual concept in the afterlife.<br /><br />The historical bits about women writers was quite interesting also &amp; does permeate throughout the novel but comes to the fore in the last part of the story arc. In history it always seems as women took baby steps toward independent thinking and actions only to be forced back into the male view of their proper place over and over again!<br /><br />As you can see there is a lot going on in this novel and I have barely scraped the surface but if you liked Snowflower &amp; the Secret Fan because of the relationships between women, you should enjoy this unusual presentation of "sisters" and mothers &amp; daughters.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13808358185223664453" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_44898699" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44898699" />
    <a title="Jen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/689546-jen"><img alt="Jen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1378419794p2/689546.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/44898699?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jen" href="/user/show/689546-jen">Jen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">those interested in a woman's role in Imperial China</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/689546-jen?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/689546-jen?shelf=truthiness">truthiness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/689546-jen?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer44898699" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9429179559580960189">Lisa See's research comes out in almost every passage of this historical novel of the first Manchu dynasty in China. Because my daughter is learning about China, I gravitated towards this novel in eagerness to learn more about the power, or lack of power, that women have had in China throughout the centuries. See follows the plot of a famous Chinese opera, The Peony Pavilion, by having her young maiden be so swept away with emotion and desire that she stops eating and loses her chance at earthly</span>
  <span id="freeText9429179559580960189" style="display:none">Lisa See's research comes out in almost every passage of this historical novel of the first Manchu dynasty in China. Because my daughter is learning about China, I gravitated towards this novel in eagerness to learn more about the power, or lack of power, that women have had in China throughout the centuries. See follows the plot of a famous Chinese opera, The Peony Pavilion, by having her young maiden be so swept away with emotion and desire that she stops eating and loses her chance at earthly happiness. Veering from the plot of the opera, she then shows how her maiden gains wisdom from the trials placed into her afterlife and eventually finds her voice by working with her earthly sister-wives.<br />If you don't have patience for speculation about the afterlife, don't bother picking this novel up since other reviewers have described it as being too "metaphysical" for their taste. And if you're all about the consummation of young love, this won't be your cup of tea either. Rather, this novel seems to be a meditation on the many different kinds of love that come into our lives. Being a lovesick maiden is well and good, but hanging around to experience the love of parenting, of mature love with a partner, of a mature relationship with your own parent, definitely has its own rewards, probably way greater than which we see between Romeo and Juliet, to mix my historical locations and stories.<br />The hidden reward for me was to discover that Imperial China had its published female writers and women of accomplishment. It was a short time in China's history, but how awesome to discover that even in repressive China, women had found a way to be heard and to show what their lives were like. This is an inspiration, and gives me an opening in conversations about the power of women when I talk with my daughters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9429179559580960189" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
